- **Purpose**: Backtesting evaluates the effectiveness of [[Lecture Notes Bonds,  Preferred Stock,  and Structured Products|portfolio strategies]],  models,  and tools under historical conditions.
- **Examples**:
  - Comparing the Svensson model with the Nelson-Siegel polynomial or cubic spline models for yield curve fitting.
  - Testing optimization techniques,  machine learning approaches,  and factor models.

#### **2.2 Challenges in Backtesting**
- **Multiple Testing Bias**:
  - Strategies that perform well in backtests may fail under real-world conditions due to overfitting or biased estimations.
- **Adjustments for Multiple Tests**:
  - Modern finance emphasizes transparency,  robust testing,  and adjustments for multiple comparisons to ensure reliable results.

### **3. Factor Investing in Sovereign Bond Markets**

#### **3.1 Overview of Factor Investing**
- **Definition**: Factor investing involves identifying and exploiting systematic sources of [[Lecture 7-Risk and Return of Bonds|risk and return]] in bond markets.
- **Challenges**:
  - Unlike equities,  sovereign bonds lack cross-sectional differences in creditworthiness,  making factor identification more complex.
- **Opportunities**:
  - Excess [[Assets|returns]] can arise from behavioral [[Week 2 Fundamentals Of Forecasting|biases]],  market inefficiencies,  or systematic risk premia.

#### **3.2 Problems with Existing Bond Benchmarks**
- **"Bums’ Problem"**:
  - Market-value-weighted bond indices overweight issuers with large amounts of outstanding debt,  leading to concentration and poor diversification.
- **Uncontrolled Risk Exposures**:
  - Fluctuations in [[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|duration]] and [[Quantitative Trading Strategies Lecture Notes|credit risk]] compromise the stability of benchmarks,  creating asset-liability mismatches for investors.

#### **3.3 Benefits and Pitfalls of Factor Investing**
- **Benefits**:
  - Identifies robust,  economically motivated sources of risk.
  - Provides a framework for constructing diversified,  risk-adjusted portfolios.
- **Pitfalls**:
  - Behavioral anomalies may disappear as they are arbitraged away.
  - Institutional frictions or regulatory changes can eliminate excess [[Assets|returns]].

### **4. Portfolio Optimization and Risk Management**

#### **4.1 Spot Curve-Based Optimization**
- **Yield Curve Modeling**:
  - Use local [[Forwards and Futures Notes|currency]] yield curves to model [[An Asset Allocation Primer|portfolio]] [[Assets|returns]] and optimize allocations.
- **Constraints**:
  - Incorporate [[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|duration]],  factor exposure,  [[Forwards and Futures Notes|currency]] exposure,  spreads,  turnover,  and specific [[An Asset Allocation Primer|portfolio]] holdings constraints.

#### **4.2 Currency Risk Management**
- **Minimum Variance Optimization (MVO)**:
  - Minimize [[Forwards and Futures Notes|currency]] risk by optimizing allocations based on the variance-covariance matrix of [[Forwards and Futures Notes|currency]] [[Assets|returns]].
- **[[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|Hedging]] Strategies**:
  - Use [[Forwards and Futures Notes|currency]] overlays and momentum techniques to manage FX exposure.

#### **4.3 Performance Attribution**
- **Yield Curve-Based Attribution**:
  - Decompose [[Assets|returns]] into roll-down,  parallel,  twist,  butterfly,  and spread effects.
  - Attribution models explain up to 97% of local [[Forwards and Futures Notes|currency]] bond [[Assets|returns]],  providing actionable insights for [[An Asset Allocation Primer|portfolio]] adjustments.

### **5. Summary and Key Takeaways**

#### **5.1 Spot Curve-Based Attribution**
- Provides detailed insights into the drivers of [[An Asset Allocation Primer|portfolio]] performance,  including roll-down,  coupon,  parallel shifts,  twists,  butterfly movements,  spread changes,  and FX effects.
- Helps managers evaluate active decisions and identify areas for improvement.

#### **5.2 Factor Investing**
- Factor investing in sovereign bond markets is still in its infancy but offers opportunities to exploit systematic risk premia and market inefficiencies.
- Robust benchmarks and economically motivated factors are essential for successful implementation.

#### **5.3 Portfolio Optimization**
- [[Teaching Note 4 Interest Rate Derivatives|Spot curve]]-based optimization and MVO are powerful tools for managing risks and maximizing [[Assets|returns]] in international bond portfolios.
- Constraints such as [[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|duration]],  [[Forwards and Futures Notes|currency]] exposure,  and turnover must be carefully managed to achieve [[An Asset Allocation Primer|portfolio]] objectives.

#### **5.4 Backtesting and Model Development**
- Backtesting is critical for evaluating strategies and models but must account for multiple testing bias and real-world conditions.
- Transparency and robust testing frameworks are essential for modern bond [[The Impact of Option Strategies in Financial  Portfolios Performance|portfolio management]].

By integrating these principles,  [[An Asset Allocation Primer|portfolio]] managers can construct resilient [[Lecture Notes Bonds,  Preferred Stock,  and Structured Products|fixed income]] portfolios that deliver superior risk-adjusted [[Assets|returns]] while adapting to changing market conditions.

This concludes the lecture notes on [[Teaching Note 4 Interest Rate Derivatives|spot curve]]-based attribution,  factor investing,  and [[The Modern Portfolio Theory and the Capm|portfolio optimization]] in [[A Practical Guide to Bonds and Swaps|fixed income markets]]. Next,  we will explore advanced techniques for integrating ESG factors and [[Week 4 Valuing Young and High‐Growth Companies|scenario analysis]] into bond [[The Impact of Option Strategies in Financial  Portfolios Performance|portfolio management]].

### **Lecture Notes: Fixed Income Factor Investing,  Carry Strategies,  and Conditional Strategies**

## **1. Introduction to Fixed Income Factor Investing**

### **1.1 Generalized Factors in Fixed Income**
- **Definition**: Generalized factors refer to systematic drivers of cross-sectional [[Assets|returns]] in [[A Practical Guide to Bonds and Swaps|fixed income markets]],  analogous to equity factors like value and momentum.
- **Challenges**:
  - **Proxy Problem**: [[Lecture Notes Bonds,  Preferred Stock,  and Structured Products|Fixed income]] factors like [[Class Note 10 Liquidity and Class Note 10 Liquidity and Liquidity Managementliquidity management|liquidity]] or value are difficult to measure or define precisely.
  - **Proxies of Proxies**: Researchers often rely on indirect measures (e.g.,  past 5-year [[Assets|returns]] as a proxy for value) to approximate latent factors.
  - **Behavioral vs. Risk-Based Explanations**: The strong correlation of factors like value and momentum across asset classes challenges both behavioral and rational risk-based models.

### **1.2 Importance of Understanding Factor Drivers**
- **Robust Benchmarks**: A clear understanding of the underlying causes of excess [[Assets|returns]] is essential for building stable,  investable factor benchmarks.
- **Latent Risk Premia**: Factors must be linked to economic risks (e.g.,  low growth,  high unemployment) to justify their excess [[Assets|returns]] as compensation for bearing risk.

## **2. Taxonomy of Fixed Income Factors**

### **2.1 Key Fixed Income Factors**
1. **Value**:
   - Analogous to the equity value factor but harder to define for bonds.
   - Proxies include past 5-year [[Assets|returns]] or deviations from fair value models.
1. **Momentum**:
   - Bonds with strong past performance tend to continue outperforming.
1. **[[Class Note 10 Liquidity and Class Note 10 Liquidity and Liquidity Managementliquidity management|Liquidity]]**:
   - Captures the premium for holding less liquid securities.
   - Examples: TIPS [[Class Note 10 Liquidity and Class Note 10 Liquidity and Liquidity Managementliquidity management|liquidity]] premium relative to nominal Treasuries.
1. **Low Risk**:
   - Low-maturity bonds and high-quality [[Class Notes 2 – Corporate Bond Contracts|Corporate Bonds]] often exhibit higher Sharpe ratios.
1. **Level,  Slope,  and Curvature**:
   - Time-series factors derived from yield curve dynamics.
   - Level: Parallel shifts in the yield curve.
   - Slope: Changes in steepness (e.g.,  10 Y-2 Y spread).
   - Curvature: Changes in the shape of the yield curve.

## **3. Carry Strategies in Sovereign Bond Markets**

### **3.1 Definition of Carry Strategies**
- **[[Currency Carry Trade|Carry Trade]]**: Involves holding a [[Chapter 4 - Futures: Hedging and Speculation|long position]] in a higher-yielding bond (e.g.,  10-year Treasury) while funding it by [[Short Selling|shorting]] a lower-yielding bond (e.g.,  1-year Treasury).
- **Formula**:
  $$ xret_{t \to t+1} = p_{t+1}^{N-1} - p_t^N - y_t^1 = \left (y_t^N - y_{t+1}^{N-1}\right)(N-1) + \left (y_t^N - y_t^1\right) $$
  - \( p_t^N \): Log price of an \( N \)-maturity bond.
  - \( y_t^N \): Yield of an \( N \)-maturity bond.

### **3.2 Empirical Findings**
- **Unconditional Profitability**:
  - Carry strategies have been profitable on average,  as shown in **Exhibit 58-1**:
	- Average [[Assets|returns]] increase with maturity (e.g.,  0.53% for 2-year bonds vs. 2.62% for 10-year bonds).
	- Sharpe ratios decline with maturity (e.g.,  0.30 for 2-year bonds vs. 0.25 for 10-year bonds).
- **[[Business Cycles- Introduction, Characteristics, and History|Business Cycle]] Dependence**:
  - Carry strategies are profitable during recessions but unprofitable during expansions,  particularly in the second half of expansions (**Exhibit 58-2**).
  - Sharpe ratios:
	- Recession: 0.82 (2-year),  0.72 (5-year),  0.59 (10-year).
	- Expansion: 0.01 (2-year),  0.06 (5-year),  0.05 (10-year).

### **3.3 Implications for Risk Premia**
- **[[Understanding the Term Structure of Interest Rates|Time-Varying Risk Premia]]**:
  - The [[Exercises|market price of risk]] must change sign across business cycles to explain the profitability of carry strategies.
- **Conditional Carry Strategies**:
  - Time-varying exposures to the level factor can enhance performance by adjusting for [[Business Cycles- Introduction, Characteristics, and History|business cycle]] phases.

## **4. Conditional Strategies Based on Level and Slope**

### **4.1 Conditional Carry Strategies**
- **Return-Predicting Factors**:
  - Use yield-curve or macro-financial variables to predict excess [[Assets|returns]].
  - Examples:
	- **Cieslak-Povala (CiP)**: Combines yield-curve variables with [[War Economies and Hyperinflation|inflation]] trends.
	- **Cochrane-Piazzesi (CP)**: Uses five forward rates as regressors.
- **Sharpe Ratios**:
  - Conditional strategies outperform unconditional strategies (**Exhibit 58-3**):
	- CiP: 0.522 (2-year) to 0.571 (10-year).
	- CP: 0.408 (2-year) to 0.467 (10-year).
	- Unconditional: 0.359 (2-year) to 0.297 (10-year).

### **4.2 Slope-Based Strategies**
- **Flattener/Steepener Trades**:
  - Flattener: Long 10-year bond,  short 2-year bond ([[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|duration]]-neutralized).
  - Steepener: Opposite of flattener.
- **Predictive Factors**:
  - Slope (10 Y-2 Y spread) and level are significant predictors of profitability.
  - Investors should:
	- Enter flatteners when yields are high and the curve is steep.
	- Enter steepeners when yields are low or the curve is inverted.

## **5. Value Factor in Fixed Income**

### **5.1 Challenges in Defining Value**
- **Equity Analogy**:
  - In equities,  value is typically measured using book-to-market ratios.
  - For bonds,  value is harder to define due to the absence of a clear "book value."
- **Proxies for Value**:
  - Past 5-year [[Assets|returns]].
  - Deviations from fair value models (e.g.,  yield relative to macroeconomic [[Week 2 Fundamentals Of Forecasting|fundamentals]]).

### **5.2 Empirical Evidence**
- **Value Everywhere**:
  - Despite challenges,  recent studies claim to find value in [[A Practical Guide to Bonds and Swaps|fixed income markets]].
  - Example: Bonds trading at a discount to fair value tend to outperform.

## **6. Summary and Key Takeaways**

### **6.1 Fixed Income Factors**
- Value,  momentum,  [[Class Note 10 Liquidity and Class Note 10 Liquidity and Liquidity Managementliquidity management|liquidity]],  and low risk are well-documented factors in [[A Practical Guide to Bonds and Swaps|fixed income markets]].
- Time-series factors (level,  slope,  curvature) explain a large portion of bond return variations.

### **6.2 Carry Strategies**
- Carry strategies are profitable on average but exhibit strong [[Business Cycles- Introduction, Characteristics, and History|business cycle]] dependence.
- Conditional carry strategies,  based on return-predicting factors,  significantly improve Sharpe ratios.

### **6.3 Slope-Based Strategies**
- Flattener and steepener trades offer opportunities to exploit changes in the yield curve's slope.
- Predictive factors like slope and level enhance the profitability of these strategies.

### **6.4 Value Factor**
- Defining value in [[Lecture Notes Bonds,  Preferred Stock,  and Structured Products|fixed income]] is challenging but essential for robust factor investing.
- Proxies like past [[Assets|returns]] and fair value deviations provide useful approximations.

### **6.5 Practical Applications**
- [[An Asset Allocation Primer|Portfolio]] managers can use conditional strategies and factor-based approaches to enhance [[Assets|returns]] and manage risks.
- Understanding the drivers of [[Lecture Notes Bonds,  Preferred Stock,  and Structured Products|fixed income]] factors is critical for building robust and stable portfolios.

By integrating these insights,  investors can navigate the complexities of [[A Practical Guide to Bonds and Swaps|fixed income markets]] and construct portfolios that deliver superior risk-adjusted [[Assets|returns]].

This concludes the lecture notes on [[Lecture Notes Bonds,  Preferred Stock,  and Structured Products|fixed income]] factor investing,  carry strategies,  and conditional strategies. Next,  we will explore advanced techniques for integrating ESG factors and [[Week 4 Valuing Young and High‐Growth Companies|scenario analysis]] into bond [[The Impact of Option Strategies in Financial  Portfolios Performance|portfolio management]].

# Lecture Notes: Advanced Fixed Income Portfolio Management Strategies

## **1. The Importance of Data Distribution and Policy Implications**

### **1.1 Understanding Data Distribution**
- **Debt Formation**:
  - Distinguish between debt directed toward **consumption** and **[[An Asset Allocation Primer|investment]]**.
  - Public and private debt distributions have different implications for economic growth and [[War Economies and Hyperinflation|inflation]].
- **Price Distribution and [[War Economies and Hyperinflation|Inflation]]**:
  - Inflationary pressures are unevenly distributed across sectors,  affecting [[Fixed Income Asset Pricing|asset pricing]] and [[An Asset Allocation Primer|portfolio]] decisions.
- **Trade Components**:
  - Analyze the distribution of **exports** and **imports** related to consumption and [[An Asset Allocation Primer|investment]] to infer economic trends.

### **1.2 Policy Implications**
- **[[Week 2 Fundamentals Of Forecasting|Forecasting]] Risk Parameters**:
  - [[An Asset Allocation Primer|Portfolio]] managers must integrate economic statistics with **political,  sociological,  and philosophical judgments**.
  - Regulatory changes,  political risk,  and participant psychology are critical inputs.
- **Big Changes in Capital Markets**:
  - Activist central banks (e.g.,  Federal Reserve,  ECB) have shifted from referees to **price setters**,  fundamentally altering [[Fixed Income Asset Pricing|asset pricing]].
  - Policies like ZIRP (Zero Interest Rate Policy) and NIRP (Negative Interest Rate Policy) have decoupled prices from traditional macroeconomic valuations.

## **2. Yield Curve Dynamics**

### **2.1 Yield Curve Overview**
- **Definition**:
  - The yield curve maps [[Interest Rate Quotations|interest rates]] for various maturities of a particular issuer.
- **Shape and Implications**:
  - **Normal Curve**: Longer maturities yield more due to [[War Economies and Hyperinflation|inflation]] and [[Quantitative Trading Strategies Lecture Notes|credit risk]].
  - **Inverted Curve**: Signals economic slowdown or recession.
  - **Flat Curve**: Indicates uncertainty or transition in [[Lecture 7-[[Lecture 7-Risk and Return of Bonds|Risk and Return]] of Bonds#7.6 [[A Preview of Alternative Formulations|Asset price]] reactions to [[The Economist Regime Change|monetary policy]] surprises|[[The Economist Regime Change|monetary policy]]]].

### **2.2 Sources of Return from Yield Curve Changes**
- **Parallel Shifts**:
  - Uniform changes in [[Interest Rate Quotations|interest rates]] across all maturities.
- **Twists**:
  - Changes in the steepness of the curve (e.g.,  10 Y-2 Y spread).
- **Butterfly Movements**:
  - Changes in curvature,  where intermediate maturities move differently than short and long maturities.

### **2.3 Sovereign Credit Risk**
- **Default and Restructuring**:
  - Sovereign defaults often lead to restructuring,  where creditors recover only a fraction of their principal.
- **Sovereign CDS**:
  - Credit Default Swaps (CDS) provide insurance against sovereign default and are a key indicator of creditworthiness.
- **Demographic Trends**:
  - Population growth affects debt servicing capacity,  with declining populations increasing sovereign [[Quantitative Trading Strategies Lecture Notes|credit risk]].

## **3. Currency Valuation and Policy Choices**

### **3.1 Currency Valuation**
- **Forward vs. Spot Rates**:
  - Differences between forward and spot rates reflect speculative motives or transaction costs.
- **Determinants**:
  - [[What Really Is the Cross-Currency Basis|Interest rate differentials]],  [[War Economies and Hyperinflation|inflation]],  trade flows,  and purchasing power parity (PPP).
- **Post-2008 Focus**:
  - Increased emphasis on relative factor productivity and net trade flows.

### **3.2 Policy Choices and Implications**
- **Exhibit S 9-3**: Policy Choices and Their Impact on Bonds and Currencies
  - **[[War Economies and Hyperinflation|Inflation]]**: Negative for bonds,  negative for [[Forwards and Futures Notes|currency]].
  - **Deflation**: Positive for bonds,  positive for [[Forwards and Futures Notes|currency]].
  - **Tax Changes**: Ambiguous effects on [[Forwards and Futures Notes|currency]],  positive or negative for bonds depending on direction.
  - **Spending Changes**: Increased spending is negative for bonds,  while decreased spending is positive.

## **4. Advanced Fixed Income Strategies**

### **4.1 Asset-Backed Credit Strategy**
- **Focus**:
  - Invest in securitized debt obligations (e.g.,  residential/commercial mortgages,  credit cards).
- **Valuation Models**:
  - Use proprietary models to analyze loan-level data,  payment delinquencies,  and collateral value.
- **[[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|Hedging]]**:
  - Hedge macro risks (e.g.,  market declines) using [[Chapter 9 Arbitrage and Hedging With Options|derivatives]] on specific tranches or indices.

### **4.2 Capital Structure Arbitrage**
- **Focus**:
  - Exploit mispricing across a company’s [[Introduction to Corporate Finance|capital structure]] (e.g.,  senior debt vs. Equity).
- **Catalyst Events**:
  - Identify events (e.g.,  refinancing,  restructuring) that may correct mispricing.
- **[[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|Hedging]]**:
  - Use company-specific hedges or broader industry-level hedges to manage risk.

### **4.3 Long/Short Credit Strategy**
- **Focus**:
  - Take long positions in undervalued debt and short positions in overvalued debt.
- **[[Exercises|Portfolio Construction]]**:
  - Combine cash [[Corporate Bonds and Loans|corporate debt]],  [[Chapter 9 Arbitrage and Hedging With Options|derivatives]] (e.g.,  CDS),  and sovereign [[Corporate Bonds and Loans|debt instruments]].
- **[[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|Hedging]]**:
  - Use equity,  credit,  and interest rate hedges to manage market beta exposure.

## **5. ESG Integration in Fixed Income**

### **5.1 Green Bonds**
- **Definition**:
  - Bonds issued to finance environmentally beneficial projects.
- **Challenges**:
  - Greenwashing and lack of standardized reporting.
- **Opportunities**:
  - Diversification and alignment with sustainability goals.

### **5.2 ESG Screening Techniques**
- **Negative Screening**:
  - Exclude sectors or companies that fail to meet ESG criteria.
- **Positive Screening**:
  - Focus on companies with superior ESG performance.
- **Norms-Based Screening**:
  - Evaluate investments against international norms (e.g.,  UN Global Compact).

## **6. Risk Management and Performance Attribution**

### **6.1 Risk Management**
- **[[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|Duration]] Management**:
  - Balance [[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|duration]] exposure across currencies and maturities.
- **[[Forwards and Futures Notes|Currency]] Risk**:
  - Use Minimum Variance Optimization (MVO) to manage FX risk.
- **[[Asset Backed Commercial Paper Understanding the Risks|Liquidity Risk]]**:
  - Diversify holdings and prioritize liquid securities.

### **6.2 Performance Attribution**
- **Yield Curve-Based Attribution**:
  - Decompose [[Assets|returns]] into roll-down,  parallel shifts,  twists,  butterfly movements,  and spread changes.
- **[[Teaching Note 4 Interest Rate Derivatives|Spot Curve]]-Based Attribution**:
  - Evaluate contributions from individual securities using fitted spot curves.

## **7. Summary and Key Takeaways**

### **7.1 Yield Curve Dynamics**
- Changes in the yield curve shape (parallel,  twist,  butterfly) are critical sources of return.
- Sovereign [[Quantitative Trading Strategies Lecture Notes|credit risk]] and demographic trends are key considerations for long-term bond investors.

### **7.2 Advanced Strategies**
- [[Asset Backed Commercial Paper Understanding the Risks|Asset-backed]] credit,  [[Introduction to Corporate Finance|capital structure]] [[Arbitrage Pricing of Derivatives|arbitrage]],  and long/short credit strategies offer opportunities to exploit mispricing and manage risk.
- ESG integration enhances [[An Asset Allocation Primer|portfolio]] sustainability and aligns with long-term [[An Asset Allocation Primer|investment]] goals.

### **7.3 Risk Management**
- Robust [[Financial Mathematics Course|risk management]] frameworks are essential for managing [[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|duration]],  [[Forwards and Futures Notes|currency]],  and [[Class Note 10 Liquidity and Class Note 10 Liquidity and Liquidity Managementliquidity management|liquidity]] risks in [[Lecture Notes Bonds,  Preferred Stock,  and Structured Products|fixed income]] portfolios.
- Performance attribution provides actionable insights for [[An Asset Allocation Primer|portfolio]] adjustments.

By integrating these advanced strategies and [[Financial Mathematics Course|risk management]] techniques,  [[An Asset Allocation Primer|portfolio]] managers can construct resilient [[Lecture Notes Bonds,  Preferred Stock,  and Structured Products|fixed income]] portfolios that deliver superior risk-adjusted [[Assets|returns]].

This concludes the lecture notes on advanced [[Lecture Notes Bonds,  Preferred Stock,  and Structured Products|fixed income]] [[The Impact of Option Strategies in Financial  Portfolios Performance|portfolio management]],  yield curve dynamics,  and ESG integration. Next,  we will explore practical applications of these concepts in [[Exercises|portfolio construction]] and optimization.

# Lecture Notes: Advanced Fixed Income Strategies and Financing Mechanisms

## **1. [[A Primer on Distressed Investing|Distressed Debt]] Investing**

### **1.1 Overview**
- **Definition**: [[HomeMax Case Study Solution|Distressed debt investing]] focuses on securities of companies experiencing financial stress,  which may or may not be in default.
- **[[An Asset Allocation Primer|Investment]] Situations**:
  1. **Balance Sheet Improvement**: Investments aimed at restructuring and improving corporate balance sheets.
  1. **Liquidation Investments**: Securities represent claims on valuable assets,  including cash.
  1. **[[Class Note 10 Liquidity and Class Note 10 Liquidity and Liquidity Managementliquidity management|Liquidity]] Investments**: Refinancing provides time for recovery.
  1. **Litigation Investments**: Value determined in court,  often involving creditor committees.
  1. **Short Positions**: Targeting deteriorating companies losing market share or facing [[Week 3 Cyclical Industries (and Advanced [[Week 2 Fundamentals Of Forecasting|Forecasting]])|cyclical]] challenges.

### **1.2 Portfolio Construction**
- **[[Introduction to Corporate Finance|Capital Structure]]**:
  - Securities range from [[Note On LBO Capital Structure Module Note|senior secured debt]] (e.g.,  bank loans) to subordinated debt (e.g.,  second lien debt) and junior securities (e.g.,  busted convertibles,  equities).
  - Specialized securities include [[Notes on Debtor-in-Possession [[Notes on Debtor-in-Possession DIP Financing|DIP Financing]]|debtor-in-possession]] (DIP) financing and rescue financing.
- **Characteristics of Securities**:
  - Industry leaders or strategically important companies.
  - Securities priced with a cushion to withstand downturns or volatility.
  - Valuation affirmed using [[BMW Valuation|peer companies]] in similar industries.
- **Collateralized Obligations**:
  - Includes impaired structured credit like CDOs and Close.
  - Valuation involves disaggregating to the loan or bond level and comparing against market [[Arbitrage Pricing of Derivatives|pricing]].

### **1.3 Tactical Adjustments**
- **Economic Cycles**:
  - **Weak Cycles**: Focus on senior secured positions.
  - **Growth Phases**: Shift to junior securities,  [[HBR Case Study- Oaktree|unsecured debt]],  or equity.
- **[[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|Hedging]]**:
  - Use of index hedges,  interest-rate hedges,  or positions in related companies.
  - Short positions in declining companies or industries.

## **2. Basis Trading**

### **2.1 Definition**
- **Concept**: Simultaneous purchase and sale of closely related securities to exploit relative mispricing.
- **Examples**:
  1. **[[Part II-Basis Trading and the Implied Repo Rate|Futures Basis]] Trade**: Buy a cash U.S. Treasury security and sell a [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|futures]] contract.
  1. **Credit [[Basis Trade Explainer|Basis Trade]]**: Buy a cash corporate bond and hedge [[Quantitative Trading Strategies Lecture Notes|credit risk]] using CDS.
  1. **[[Table of Contents|Cross-Currency Basis Swap]]**: Fund in one [[Forwards and Futures Notes|currency]] and hedge via [[Forwards and Futures Notes|currency]] forward markets.

### **2.2 Futures Basis Trade**
- **Key Factor**: Delivery option in [[Mathematics of the Financial Markets|futures contracts]].
- **Net Basis**:
  - Adjusted for net carry ([[Pl Attribution|coupon income]] minus funding cost).
  - Positive net basis implies a negative [[Lecture 1- Probability Distributions of Returns|expected return]] due to the delivery option's value.
- **Volatility Impact**:
  - Rising interest-rate volatility increases the value of the delivery option,  benefiting the short [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|futures]] position.

### **2.3 Cash-CDS Basis Trade**
- **[[Class Note 10 Liquidity and Class Note 10 Liquidity and Liquidity Managementliquidity management|Liquidity]] Factor**:
  - During crises (e.g.,  2008 GFC),  cash bonds traded wider than CDS,  creating [[Class Note 13 The LTCM Meltdown|arbitrage opportunities]].
  - Ample [[Class Note 10 Liquidity and Class Note 10 Liquidity and Liquidity Managementliquidity management|liquidity]] narrows the basis,  reducing profitability.

## **3. Volatility Trading**

### **3.1 Overview**
- **Focus**: Trading [[A Real-Life Option Pricing Exercise|implied volatility]] in [[Lecture Notes Bonds,  Preferred Stock,  and Structured Products|fixed income]] options markets.
- **Strategies**:
  1. **[[Implied Vs Realised Convexity|Implied vs. Realized]] Volatility**:
	 - Sell options when [[A Real-Life Option Pricing Exercise|implied volatility]] exceeds forecasted realized volatility.
	 - Delta-hedge the underlying risk.
  1. **Variance and Volatility Swaps**:
	 - Direct bets on volatility levels relative to a strike.
  1. **[[7. Black Scholes Model|Volatility Surface]] [[Arbitrage Pricing of Derivatives|Arbitrage]]**:
	 - Exploit mispricing across strikes,  expiries,  and maturities.
  1. **Cross-Market [[Arbitrage Pricing of Derivatives|Arbitrage]]**:
	 - Trade volatility across markets (e.g.,  U.S. vs. Europe,  [[Lecture Notes Bonds,  Preferred Stock,  and Structured Products|fixed income]] vs. [[Forwards and Futures Notes|Currency]] options).

### **3.2 Applications**
- **[[Fremont Financial Corp. (b)|Mortgage-Backed Securities]] (MBS)**:
  - Rising volatilities increase prepayment probabilities,  affecting [[Mortgage Pricing Spreads and Duration|MBS pricing]].
  - [[Class Note 13 The LTCM Meltdown|Arbitrage opportunities]] arise when swaption volatilities differ from MBS-implied volatilities.
- **Directional Trades**:
  - Sell payer swaptions at high strikes if [[A Real-Life Option Pricing Exercise|implied volatility]] is overpriced due to [[War Economies and Hyperinflation|inflation]] [[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|hedging]] demand.

## **4. Cross-Currency Arbitrage**

### **4.1 Strategy**
- **Concept**: Purchase foreign bonds and hedge [[Forwards and Futures Notes|currency]] risk using cross-[[Financial Instruments PSET Solutions|currency swaps]].
- **Mechanics**:
  - Hedge [[Forwards and Futures Notes|currency]] risk with shorter maturity [[Forwards and Futures|forwards]] than the bond's maturity.
  - Yield enhancement arises from [[The Gauss Model|short-term interest rate]] differentials.

### **4.2 Risks**
1. **[[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|Duration]] Risk**: Long maturities of underlying bonds.
1. **[[Forwards and Futures Notes|Currency]] [[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|Hedging]] Risk**: Inability to roll forward contracts.
1. **[[Quantitative Trading Strategies Lecture Notes|Credit Risk]]**: Spread widening or default of the underlying bond.

### **4.3 Applications**
- **Peripheral European Bonds**:
  - Foreign buyers hedge [[Forwards and Futures Notes|currency]] risk and take on [[Quantitative Trading Strategies Lecture Notes|credit risk]] for higher yields.
- **Yield Enhancement**:
  - [[Forwards and Futures Notes|Currency]]-hedged foreign bonds can offer higher yields than domestic bonds for short-term investors.

## **5. Financing Positions in the Bond Market**

### **5.1 Collateralized Loans**
- **Mechanisms**:
  1. **[[Class Note 12 Part 2 Repos|[[Class Slides On Repurchase Agreements|repurchase agreements]]]] (Repos)**:
	 - Sale of a security with a commitment to repurchase at a specified price and date.
	 - Repo rate determines the cost of borrowing.
  1. **[[Dollar Rolls|Dollar Rolls]]**:
	 - Sale and repurchase of MBS,  with delivery deferred to a future date.
  1. **Securities Lending**:
	 - Loan of securities in exchange for collateral.
  1. **Margin Buying**:
	 - Borrowing funds to purchase securities,  using the securities as collateral.

### **5.2 Repo Agreements**
- **Example**:
  - Borrow \$10,  015,  455 for 10 days at a repo rate of 0.08%.
  - Repurchase price: \$10,  015,  677.57.
  - Dollar interest: \$222.57.
- **Haircuts**:
  - Margin (1%–3%) reduces [[Quantitative Trading Strategies Lecture Notes|credit risk]] by requiring collateral value to exceed loan value.
  - Example with 2% haircut:
	- Loan amount: \$9,  819,  073.53.
	- Interest: \$218.20.
	- Repurchase price: \$9,  819,  291.73.

## **6. Summary and Key Takeaways**

### **6.1 [[A Primer on Distressed Investing|Distressed Debt]]**
- Focuses on securities of financially stressed companies,  with opportunities across the [[Introduction to Corporate Finance|capital structure]].
- Tactical adjustments and [[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|hedging]] strategies align with economic cycles.

### **6.2 Basis Trading**
- Exploits relative mispricing between closely related securities.
- [[Class Note 10 Liquidity and Class Note 10 Liquidity and Liquidity Managementliquidity management|Liquidity]] and volatility are key drivers of profitability.

### **6.3 Volatility Trading**
- Trades [[A Real-Life Option Pricing Exercise|implied volatility]] through options,  variance swaps,  and cross-market [[Arbitrage Pricing of Derivatives|arbitrage]].
- MBS and swaptions provide unique opportunities for volatility [[Arbitrage Pricing of Derivatives|arbitrage]].

### **6.4 Cross-Currency Arbitrage**
- Enhances yields by leveraging [[What Really Is the Cross-Currency Basis|interest rate differentials]] and [[Forward Rate|hedging currency risk]].
- [[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|Duration]],  [[Forwards and Futures Notes|currency]],  and credit risks must be carefully managed.

### **6.5 Financing Mechanisms**
- Collateralized loans (repos,  [[Dollar Rolls|dollar rolls]]) and [[Chapter 9 Arbitrage and Hedging With Options|derivatives]] provide [[Lecture 6-Leverage, Tail Risk, Volatility Products|leverage]] and [[Class Note 10 Liquidity and Class Note 10 Liquidity and Liquidity Managementliquidity management|liquidity]].
- Haircuts and [[Lecture 6-[[Lecture 6-Leverage, Tail Risk, Volatility Products|Leverage]], Tail Risk, Volatility Products#6.1.3 [[Case Study Mf Globals Repo-To-Maturity Trades|Margin requirements]]|[[Case Study Mf Globals Repo-To-Maturity Trades|margin requirements]]]] mitigate [[Quantitative Trading Strategies Lecture Notes|credit risk]].

By integrating these strategies and financing mechanisms,  [[An Asset Allocation Primer|portfolio]] managers can optimize risk-adjusted [[Assets|returns]] while navigating the complexities of [[A Practical Guide to Bonds and Swaps|fixed income markets]].

This concludes the lecture notes on advanced [[Lecture Notes Bonds,  Preferred Stock,  and Structured Products|fixed income]] strategies,  including [[A [[An Asset Allocation Primer|Primer]] on [[HomeMax Case Study Solution|Distressed Investing]]|distressed debt]],  basis trading,  volatility trading,  cross-[[The Foreign Exchange Market Annotations|currency arbitrage]],  and financing mechanisms. Next,  we will explore practical applications of these concepts in [[Exercises|portfolio construction]] and optimization.

# Lecture Notes: Repo Agreements,  Dollar Rolls,  Margin Buying,  Securities Lending,  and Interest Rate Derivatives

## **1. Repo Agreements**

### **1.1 Overview**
- **Definition**: A repurchase agreement (repo) is the sale of a security with a commitment by the seller to buy it back at a specified price on a designated future date.
- **Key Features**:
  - **Repo Margin (Haircut)**: The amount by which the security's value exceeds the loan amount,  providing a cushion against market fluctuations.
  - **Mark-to-Market**: Positions are marked to market daily,  and [[Class Note 13 The LTCM Meltdown|margin calls]] are issued if the collateral value falls below the required level.
  - **Delivery of Collateral**:
	- **Delivered Out**: Collateral is physically delivered to the lender.
	- **Hold-in-Custody (HIC) Repo**: Borrower retains the collateral in a segregated account,  increasing [[Quantitative Trading Strategies Lecture Notes|credit risk]].
	- **Triparty Repo**: Collateral is held in a custodial account at the borrower's clearing bank,  reducing delivery costs.

### **1.2 Determinants of the Repo Rate**
- **Quality of Collateral**: Higher-quality collateral (e.g.,  Treasuries) results in lower [[Implied Repo Rates|repo rates]].
- **Term of the Repo**: Longer terms may carry higher rates due to increased risk.
- **Delivery of Collateral**: Delivered-out [[Class Note 12 Part 2 Repos|repos]] may have lower rates due to reduced [[Quantitative Trading Strategies Lecture Notes|credit risk]].
- **Availability of Collateral**: Scarce collateral increases demand,  lowering [[Implied Repo Rates|repo rates]].
- **[[Topics in Fiscal and Monetary Policies and Stabilization- Empirical Issues|Federal Funds Rate]]**: [[Implied Repo Rates|Repo rates]] are influenced by prevailing short-term [[Interest Rate Quotations|interest rates]].

## **2. Dollar Rolls**

### **2.1 Overview**
- **Definition**: A specialized type of collateralized loan in the [[Fremont Financial Corp. (b)|mortgage-backed securities]] (MBS) market,  where the dealer borrows securities and [[Assets|returns]] "substantially identical securities."
- **Key Features**:
  - **Flexibility**: Dealer need not return the exact securities but must match coupon rate,  issuer,  and collateral type.
  - **100% Financing**: No overcollateralization required.
  - **Coupon and Principal Payments**: Dealer retains coupon and principal payments during the loan period.

### **2.2 Determination of Financing Cost**
- **Key Elements**:
  1. Sale and repurchase prices.
  1. [[Realized Returns|Coupon payments]] retained by the dealer.
  1. Scheduled and prepayment principal repayments.
  1. Attributes of the returned securities.
  1. Delivery tolerances for underor overdelivery.
- **Drop**: The difference between the sale and repurchase prices,  representing the [[Realized Returns|financing cost]].

## **3. Margin Buying**

### **3.1 Overview**
- **Definition**: Borrowing cash to buy securities,  using the purchased securities as collateral.
- **Key Features**:
  - **Call Money Rate**: The interest rate charged by banks to brokers for margin loans.
  - **Initial Margin Requirement**: Set by the Federal Reserve under Regulation T,  currently 50% for stocks and non-government bonds.
  - **Maintenance Margin Requirement**: Minimum equity required in the margin account; failure to meet it results in a margin call.

## **4. Securities Lending**

### **4.1 Overview**
- **Definition**: A transaction where the owner of a security lends it to another party in exchange for collateral.
- **Key Features**:
  - **Collateral Types**: Cash,  letters of credit,  or securities.
  - **Mark-to-Market**: Collateral is marked to market daily.
  - **Reinvestment Risk**: When cash is used as collateral,  the lender reinvests it and earns a fee (rebate rate).
  - **Substitute Payments**: Borrower compensates the lender for any interest or dividends paid during the loan period.

### **4.2 Comparison to Repo Agreements**
- **Similarities**:
  - Both are secured borrowing arrangements.
  - Both involve collateral and cash flows.
- **Differences**:
  - Securities lending does not have a fixed repurchase price or date.
  - Securities lending often involves substitute payments for income generated by the collateral.

## **5. Interest Rate Derivatives**

### **5.1 Futures Contracts**
- **Definition**: Standardized agreements to buy or sell a financial instrument at a specified price on a future date.
- **Key Features**:
  - **Mark-to-Market**: Gains and losses are settled daily.
  - **[[Lecture 6-[[Lecture 6-Leverage, Tail Risk, Volatility Products|Leverage]], Tail Risk, Volatility Products#6.1.3 [[Case Study Mf Globals Repo-To-Maturity Trades|Margin requirements]]|[[Case Study Mf Globals Repo-To-Maturity Trades|margin requirements]]]]**: Initial and maintenance margins are required.
  - **Settlement**: Can be physical delivery or [[Swaptions|cash settlement]].

### **5.2 Forward Contracts**
- **Definition**: Non-standardized agreements for future delivery of an asset at a specified price.
- **Key Features**:
  - **Customization**: Terms are negotiated between buyer and seller.
  - **[[Quantitative Trading Strategies Lecture Notes|Credit Risk]]**: Both parties are exposed to counterparty risk.
  - **No Interim Cash Flows**: Unlike [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|futures]],  [[Forwards and Futures|forwards]] are not marked to market.

### **5.3 Option Contracts**
- **Definition**: Contracts granting the buyer the right,  but not the obligation,  to buy (call) or sell (put) an asset at a specified price within a specified period.
- **Key Features**:
  - **Premium**: The cost of the option paid by the buyer to the seller.
  - **[[Call and Put Payoffs at Expiry|Strike Price]]**: The price at which the option can be exercised.
  - **Expiration**: Options can be American (exercisable anytime),  European (exercisable only at expiration),  or Bermudan (exercisable on specific dates).

## **6. Summary and Key Takeaways**

### **6.1 Repo Agreements**
- [[Class Note 12 Part 2 Repos|Repos]] are short-term secured loans with collateral delivery options (delivered-out,  HIC,  triparty).
- [[Implied Repo Rates|Repo rates]] depend on collateral quality,  term,  delivery,  and market conditions.

### **6.2 Dollar Rolls**
- [[Dollar Rolls|Dollar rolls]] provide flexibility in the [[Teaching Note 6 Mortgage Backed Securities|MBS market]],  allowing dealers to return "substantially identical securities."
- Financing costs depend on sale/repurchase prices,  coupon/principal payments,  and delivery tolerances.

### **6.3 Margin Buying**
- Margin buying allows investors to [[Lecture 6-Leverage, Tail Risk, Volatility Products|leverage]] their positions but is subject to initial and maintenance [[Lecture 6-[[Lecture 6-Leverage, Tail Risk, Volatility Products|Leverage]], Tail Risk, Volatility Products#6.1.3 [[Case Study Mf Globals Repo-To-Maturity Trades|Margin requirements]]|[[Case Study Mf Globals Repo-To-Maturity Trades|margin requirements]]]].

### **6.4 Securities Lending**
- Securities lending involves lending securities in exchange for collateral,  with substitute payments for income generated by the collateral.

### **6.5 Interest Rate Derivatives**
- [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|Futures]],  [[Forwards and Futures|forwards]],  and options provide tools for [[Lecture Notes 4-Mechanics of Futures Trading and Fixed Income Derivatives|managing interest rate risk]] and enhancing [[An Asset Allocation Primer|portfolio]] performance.
- [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|Futures]] are standardized and marked to market,  while [[Forwards and Futures|forwards]] are customized and carry [[Quantitative Trading Strategies Lecture Notes|credit risk]].
- Options offer asymmetric risk/reward profiles,  with calls benefiting from price increases and puts benefiting from price declines.

By understanding these instruments and strategies,  [[An Asset Allocation Primer|portfolio]] managers can effectively manage risk,  optimize [[Assets|returns]],  and navigate the complexities of [[A Practical Guide to Bonds and Swaps|fixed income markets]].

This concludes the lecture notes on repo agreements,  [[Dollar Rolls|dollar rolls]],  margin buying,  securities lending,  and interest rate [[Chapter 9 Arbitrage and Hedging With Options|derivatives]]. Next,  we will explore advanced applications of these concepts in [[Exercises|portfolio construction]] and [[Financial Mathematics Course|risk management]].

Three-month [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] rate. For example,  if the three-month [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] rate is 2.50%,  the [[Convexity-Adjusted Models for LIBOR Forwards Qu|Eurodollar futures]] price would be quoted as 97.50 (100 - 2.50). This [[Arbitrage Pricing of Derivatives|pricing]] convention is similar to that of Treasury bill [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|futures]],  where the [[Futures Price and the Quality Option Before E|futures price]] reflects the implied interest rate.

## **1. Eurodollar Futures Contract**

### **1.1 Overview**
- **Definition**: [[Convexity-Adjusted Models for LIBOR Forwards Qu|Eurodollar futures]] are contracts based on the interest rate of U.S. dollar-denominated time deposits held in banks outside the United States.
- **Underlying Instrument**: Three-month LIBOR,  which represents the interest rate at which banks lend to each other in the London interbank market.
- **Contract Size**: $1,  000,  000 face value.
- **Settlement**: Cash-settled based on the three-month [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] rate at contract expiration.

### **1.2 Pricing and Quotation**
- **Index Price Basis**:
  - [[Futures Price and the Quality Option Before E|Futures price]] = \( 100 - \text{[[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] rate} \).
  - Example: If [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] is 3.00%,  the [[Futures Price and the Quality Option Before E|futures price]] is \( 100 - 3.00 = 97.00 \).
- **[[Duration|Price Sensitivity]]**:
  - A 0.01% (1 basis point) change in the [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] rate results in a $25 change in the contract's value.
  - This is calculated as:
	$$ \Delta \text{Value} = \text{Contract Size} \times \Delta \text{Rate} \times \frac{\text{Days to Maturity}}{360} $$
	For a 90-day contract,  \( \Delta \text{Value} = 1,  000,  000 \times 0.0001 \times \frac{90}{360} = 25 \).

### **1.3 Applications**
- **[[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|Hedging]]**:
  - Used by banks,  corporations,  and [[Chapter 4 Institutional Trading|institutional investors]] to hedge [[Analysis of Fixed Income Securities|interest rate risk]] on short-term liabilities or assets.
  - Example: A bank expecting to borrow funds in three months can lock in the borrowing cost by selling [[Convexity-Adjusted Models for LIBOR Forwards Qu|Eurodollar futures]].
- **Speculation**:
  - Traders can speculate on changes in short-term [[Interest Rate Quotations|interest rates]] by taking long or short positions in [[Convexity-Adjusted Models for LIBOR Forwards Qu|Eurodollar futures]].
- **[[Arbitrage Pricing of Derivatives|Arbitrage]]**:
  - [[Class Note 13 The LTCM Meltdown|Arbitrage opportunities]] arise when discrepancies exist between the [[Convexity-Adjusted Models for LIBOR Forwards Qu|Eurodollar futures]] price and the implied [[Forward Points in Currency|forward rate]] derived from the spot [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] curve.

### **1.4 Transition from [[A Guide to the Front End and Basis Swap Markets#London Interbank Offered Rate (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] to SOFR**
- **[[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] Phase-Out**:
  - [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] will be phased out as a reference rate after December 2021 due to concerns about its reliability and manipulation.
- **Replacement with SOFR**:
  - The [[SOFR|Secured Overnight Financing Rate]] (SOFR) is the preferred alternative,  as it is based on actual transactions in the U.S. Treasury repurchase market.
- **[[One-Month SOFR Futures|SOFR Futures]]**:
  - Similar to [[Convexity-Adjusted Models for LIBOR Forwards Qu|Eurodollar futures]],  [[One-Month SOFR Futures|SOFR futures]] are quoted on an index price basis and are [[Options Contract - Types of Contracts|used for hedging and speculation]] in the [[The Gauss Model|short-term interest rate]] market.

## **2. Treasury Bill Futures Contract**

### **2.1 Overview**
- **Definition**: Treasury bill (T-bill) [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|futures]] are contracts based on three-month U.S. Treasury bills with a face value of $1,  000,  000.
- **[[Arbitrage Pricing of Derivatives|Pricing]] Convention**:
  - [[Futures Price and the Quality Option Before E|Futures price]] = \( 100 - \text{implied interest rate} \).
  - Example: If the implied interest rate is 2.50%,  the [[Futures Price and the Quality Option Before E|futures price]] is \( 100 - 2.50 = 97.50 \).

### **2.2 Key Features**
- **[[Accrued Interest|Invoice Price]]**:
  - The actual price paid by the buyer is calculated as:
	$$ \text{Invoice Price} = 1,     000,     000 \times \left[ 1 - \text{Rate} \times \frac{\text{Days to Maturity}}{360} \right] $$
  - Example: For a 90-day T-bill with a rate of 2.50%,  the [[Accrued Interest|invoice price]] is:
	$$ 1,     000,     000 \times \left[ 1 - 0.025 \times \frac{90}{360} \right] = 993,     750 $$
- **Tick Value**:
  - A 0.01 change in the [[Futures Price and the Quality Option Before E|futures price]] corresponds to a $25 change in the contract's value.

### **2.3 Applications**
- **[[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|Hedging]]**:
  - Used to hedge [[The Gauss Model|short-term interest rate]] risk,  such as funding costs or [[Preview of the Book|cash flow]] timing mismatches.
- **Speculation**:
  - Traders can take positions based on their [[FORWARD RATES AND TERM STRUCTURE|expectations]] of changes in short-term [[Interest Rate Quotations|interest rates]].
- **[[Class Note 10 Liquidity and Class Note 10 Liquidity and Liquidity Managementliquidity management|Liquidity]]**:
  - T-bill [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|futures]] are highly liquid and serve as a benchmark for short-term [[Interest Rate Quotations|interest rates]].

## **3. Comparison of Eurodollar and Treasury Bill Futures**

| Feature | [[Convexity-Adjusted Models for LIBOR Forwards Qu|Eurodollar Futures]] | Treasury Bill [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|Futures]] |

| **Underlying Instrument** | Three-month [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] | Three-month U.S. Treasury bills |

| **Contract Size** | $1,     000,     000                            | $1,  000,  000 |

| **Settlement** | Cash | Physical |

| **[[Arbitrage Pricing of Derivatives|Pricing]] Basis** | \( 100 - \text{[[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] rate} \) | \( 100 - \text{implied interest rate} \) |

| **Tick Value** | $25 per basis point                   | $25 per basis point |

| **Primary Use** | [[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|Hedging]] short-term funding costs | [[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|Hedging]] [[The Gauss Model|short-term interest rate]] risk |

## **4. Summary and Key Takeaways**

### **4.1 Eurodollar Futures**
- [[Convexity-Adjusted Models for LIBOR Forwards Qu|Eurodollar futures]] are widely used for [[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|hedging]] and speculating on short-term [[Interest Rate Quotations|interest rates]].
- The transition from [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] to SOFR represents a significant shift in the [[The Gauss Model|short-term interest rate]] market.

### **4.2 Treasury Bill Futures**
- Treasury bill [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|futures]] provide a straightforward means of [[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|hedging]] or speculating on the short end of the yield curve.
- Their simplicity and [[Class Note 10 Liquidity and Class Note 10 Liquidity and Liquidity Managementliquidity management|liquidity]] make them a popular choice for market participants.

### **4.3 Practical Applications**
- Both Eurodollar and Treasury bill [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|futures]] are essential tools for [[Lecture Notes 4-Mechanics of Futures Trading and Fixed Income Derivatives|managing interest rate risk]] and optimizing [[An Asset Allocation Primer|portfolio]] performance.
- Understanding the [[Arbitrage Pricing of Derivatives|pricing]] conventions and applications of these contracts is critical for effective [[Financial Mathematics Course|risk management]].

By leveraging these instruments,  [[An Asset Allocation Primer|portfolio]] managers can navigate the complexities of [[The Gauss Model|short-term interest rate]] markets and achieve their [[An Asset Allocation Primer|investment]] objectives.

This concludes the lecture notes on [[Convexity-Adjusted Models for LIBOR Forwards Qu|Eurodollar futures]],  Treasury bill [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|futures]],  and their applications in [[Lecture Notes Bonds,  Preferred Stock,  and Structured Products|fixed income]] [[The Impact of Option Strategies in Financial  Portfolios Performance|portfolio management]]. Next,  we will explore advanced techniques for integrating these instruments into [[The Modern Portfolio Theory and the Capm|portfolio optimization]] and [[Financial Mathematics Course|risk management]] frameworks.

### **Lecture Notes: Eurodollar Futures,  SOFR Futures,  [[A Guide to the Front End and Basis Swap Markets#Federal Funds Market|Fed Funds]] Futures,  and Advanced Interest Rate Futures Contracts**

## **1. Eurodollar Futures**

### **1.1 Overview**
- **Definition**: [[Convexity-Adjusted Models for LIBOR Forwards Qu|Eurodollar futures]] are contracts based on the interest rate of U.S. dollar-denominated time deposits held in banks outside the United States.
- **Underlying Instrument**: Three-month [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] ([[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]]).
- **Contract Size**: $1,  000,  000 face value.
- **Settlement**: Cash-settled based on the three-month [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] rate at contract expiration.

### **1.2 Pricing and Quotation**
- **Index Price Basis**:
  - [[Futures Price and the Quality Option Before E|Futures price]] = \( 100 - \text{[[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] rate} \).
  - Example: If [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] is 2.50%,  the [[Futures Price and the Quality Option Before E|futures price]] is \( 100 - 2.50 = 97.50 \).
- **Tick Value**:
  - A 0.01% (1 basis point) change in the [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] rate results in a $25 change in the contract's value:
	$$ \Delta \text{Value} = \$1,  000,  000 \times 0.0001 \times \frac{90}{360} = \$25 $$
- **Minimum Tick Size**:
  - The minimum price fluctuation (tick) is 0.005 or ½ basis point,  with a tick value of $12.50:
	$$ \text{Tick Value} = \$1,  000,  000 \times (0.005 \times \frac{90}{360}) = \$12.50 $$

### **1.3 Example: Profit/Loss Calculation**
- **Scenario**:
  - An investor buys one [[Convexity-Adjusted Models for LIBOR Forwards Qu|Eurodollar futures]] contract at a price of 98.00 (implying a [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] rate of 2.00%).
  - One week later,  the [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] rate increases by 20 basis points to 2.20%,  causing the [[Futures Price and the Quality Option Before E|futures price]] to decline to 97.80.
- **Loss Calculation**:
  - Change in price = \( 98.00 - 97.80 = 0.20 \).
  - Loss = \( \$1,  000,  000 \times 0.20\% \times \frac{90}{360} = \$500 \).
  - Alternatively,  using tick value:
	- 20 basis points = \( 20 \times \$25 = -\$500 \).

### **1.4 Packs and Bundles**
- **Packs**:
  - Groups of four quarterly [[Convexity-Adjusted Models for LIBOR Forwards Qu|Eurodollar futures]] contracts traded together.
  - Examples:
	- **White Pack**: First four quarterly contracts (e.g.,  Jun 20,  Sep 20,  Dec 20,  Mar 21).
	- **Red Pack**: Next four quarterly contracts (e.g.,  Jun 21,  Sep 21,  Dec 21,  Mar 22).
  - Value of 1 basis point = \( 4 \times \$25 = \$100 \).
- **Bundles**:
  - Groups of multiple packs traded together.
  - Examples:
	- **White Bundle**: First four contracts (\$100 per basis point).
	- **Red Bundle**: First eight contracts (\$200 per basis point).
	- **Green Bundle**: First 12 contracts (\$300 per basis point).
	- **Copper Bundle**: First 40 contracts (\$1,  000 per basis point).

### **1.5 Transition from [[A Guide to the Front End and Basis Swap Markets#London Interbank Offered Rate (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] to SOFR**
- **[[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] Phase-Out**:
  - [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] will be replaced by SOFR ([[SOFR|Secured Overnight Financing Rate]]) due to reliability concerns.
- **[[One-Month SOFR Futures|SOFR Futures]]**:
  - Similar to [[Convexity-Adjusted Models for LIBOR Forwards Qu|Eurodollar futures]] but based on compounded daily SOFR interest during the reference quarter.
  - Price = \( 100 - \text{SOFR rate} \).
  - Tick value = $25 per basis point.

## **2. SOFR Interest Rate Futures**

### **2.1 Overview**
- **Definition**: [[Mathematics of the Financial Markets|Futures contracts]] based on the [[SOFR|Secured Overnight Financing Rate]] (SOFR),  a benchmark interest rate for [[Class Slides On Repurchase Agreements|repurchase agreements]].
- **Underlying Instrument**: Compounded daily SOFR interest during the reference quarter.
- **Contract Size**: $1,  000,  000 face value.
- **Settlement**: Cash-settled.

### **2.2 Pricing and Quotation**
- **Index Price Basis**:
  - [[Futures Price and the Quality Option Before E|Futures price]] = \( 100 - \text{SOFR rate} \).
- **Tick Value**:
  - A 1 basis point change in the SOFR rate results in a $25 change in the contract's value.

## **3. [[A Guide to the Front End and Basis Swap Markets#Federal Funds Market|Fed Funds]] Futures**

### **3.1 Overview**
- **Definition**: [[Mathematics of the Financial Markets|Futures contracts]] based on the average overnight [[Topics in Fiscal and Monetary Policies and Stabilization- Empirical Issues|federal funds rate]] for the [[One-Month SOFR Futures|delivery month]].
- **Underlying Instrument**: Simple average overnight [[Topics in Fiscal and Monetary Policies and Stabilization- Empirical Issues|federal funds rate]].
- **Contract Size**: $5,  000,  000 notional value.
- **Settlement**: Cash-settled on the last business day of the month.

### **3.2 Pricing and Quotation**
- **Index Price Basis**:
  - [[Futures Price and the Quality Option Before E|Futures price]] = \( 100 - \text{[[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#Federal Funds Market|[[Fed Fund Futures|Fed Funds]] Rate]] } \).
- **Tick Value**:
  - A 1 basis point change in the [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#Federal Funds Market|[[Fed Fund Futures|Fed Funds]] Rate]]  results in a $41.67 change in the contract's value:
	$$ \Delta \text{Value} = \$5,  000,  000 \times 0.0001 \times \frac{30}{360} = \$41.67 $$

## **4. Swap Futures Contracts**

### **4.1 Overview**
- **Definition**: [[Mathematics of the Financial Markets|Futures contracts]] based on the notional price of the fixed-rate side of a 10-year [[Primer on Interest Rate Swaps|interest rate swap]].
- **Underlying Instrument**: 10-year [[Primer on Interest Rate Swaps|interest rate swap]] with a [[Fundamentals of Swaps|notional principal]] of $100,  000.
- **Settlement**: Cash-settled based on the ISDA benchmark 10-year [[Teaching Note 4 Interest Rate Derivatives|swap rate]].

### **4.2 Applications**
- **[[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|Hedging]]**:
  - Used to hedge [[Analysis of Fixed Income Securities|interest rate risk]] in swap portfolios.
- **Speculation**:
  - Traders can speculate on changes in swap rates.

## **5. Other Futures Contracts**

### **5.1 Treasury Futures**
- **On-the-Run (OTR) Treasury [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|Futures]]**:
  - Based on the most recently auctioned 2-year,  5-year,  and 10-year Treasury notes.
  - Cash-settled with a notional value of $100,  000.

### **5.2 Credit Futures**
- **Barclays U.S. Aggregate Bond Index [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|Futures]]**:
  - Based on the Barclays Capital U.S. Aggregate Bond Index.
  - Includes Treasury,  agency,  corporate,  mortgage-backed,  [[Asset Backed Commercial Paper Understanding the Risks|asset-backed]],  and commercial [[Fremont Financial Corp. (b)|mortgage-backed securities]].

## **6. Summary and Key Takeaways**

### **6.1 Eurodollar Futures**
- [[Convexity-Adjusted Models for LIBOR Forwards Qu|Eurodollar futures]] are widely used for [[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|hedging]] and speculating on short-term [[Interest Rate Quotations|interest rates]].
- Packs and bundles provide exposure to different segments of the yield curve.

### **6.2 SOFR Futures**
- [[One-Month SOFR Futures|SOFR futures]] are the successor to [[Convexity-Adjusted Models for LIBOR Forwards Qu|Eurodollar futures]],  reflecting the transition from [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#[[Short-Term Rates and the Transition from LIBOR|London Interbank Offered Rate]] (LIBOR)|LIBOR]] to SOFR.

### **6.3 [[A Guide to the Front End and Basis Swap Markets#Federal Funds Market|Fed Funds]] Futures**
- [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#Federal Funds Market|[[Fed Fund Futures|Fed Funds]]]] [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|futures]] allow market participants to hedge or speculate on changes in the [[Topics in Fiscal and Monetary Policies and Stabilization- Empirical Issues|federal funds rate]].

### **6.4 Swap Futures**
- Swap [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|futures]] provide exposure to interest rate swaps and are [[Options Contract - Types of Contracts|used for hedging and speculation]].

### **6.5 Treasury and Credit Futures**
- Treasury [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|futures]] provide synthetic exposure to benchmark maturities on the U.S. Treasury yield curve.
- Credit [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|futures]] allow for [[Key Rates O1s Durations and Hedging|hedging]] and speculation on credit spreads and bond market performance.

By understanding these instruments,  [[An Asset Allocation Primer|portfolio]] managers can effectively manage [[Analysis of Fixed Income Securities|interest rate risk]],  optimize [[An Asset Allocation Primer|portfolio]] performance,  and navigate the complexities of [[A Practical Guide to Bonds and Swaps|fixed income markets]].

This concludes the lecture notes on [[Convexity-Adjusted Models for LIBOR Forwards Qu|Eurodollar futures]],  [[One-Month SOFR Futures|SOFR futures]],  [[A Guide to the Front End and [[Basis Swaps|Basis Swap]] Markets#Federal Funds Market|[[Fed Fund Futures|Fed Funds]]]] [[Futures Not Subject to Cash-And-Carry|futures]],  and advanced [[Chapter 12 - Hedging with Interest Rate Futures|interest rate futures]] contracts. Next,  we will explore practical applications of these instruments in [[Exercises|portfolio construction]] and [[Financial Mathematics Course|risk management]].
